The Minstrel Boy

Do thriall chum chatha óg-laoch na rann,
lár námhad Éireann ársaighe;
lann athar fáisgthe air go teann,
ann aonfheacht le n-a chláirsigh.
‘A thír na n-dán!’ ar an laoch-cheol grinn,
‘dá mbeidheadh an saoghal dod’ dhaoradh
tá aon chruit amháin le do mholadh go binn,
’s aon lann amháin le do shaoradh!’
(Deireadh Fómhair 1881, 4)
<L 43>
Barney Mavourneen I Won’t Let You In

’Twas a cold winter’s night and the winds were a snarling,
The snow like a sheet, covered cabin and stile,
When Barney flew over the hills to his darling,
He tapped at the window where Katty did lie,
i ‘Achusla’, said he, “are you sleeping or waking,
’Tis a bitter cold night, and my coat is so thin,
The storm is a brewing, the frost is a breaking,
O! Kathleen Mavoureen I pray let me in.

‘Achusla’, said Kate, as she spoke through the window,
‘Why would you be taking us out of our bed,
To come at this time ’tis a shame and a sin too,
’Tis whiskey not love that’s got into your head;
If your heart would be true of my fame you’d be tender,
What has a poor girl but her name to defend her,

Then Barney Mavourneen I won’t let you in … ’

(Iúil 1882, 95)

Ní raibh in obair na Gaeilge ach cuid den obair náisiúnta a mheas
An Gaodhal a bheith ar siúl aici agus léiríodh an dearcadh a bhí ag an
iris ar an obair náisiúnta sin sna blúirí eolais, agus sna ‘Sgeulta Ó’n
t-Sean Tír’ a fhoilsítí san iris go rialta:

Tá buaireadh orrainn nach bh-fuil an nuadhacht as an t-sean tír
timchioll dligheamh nuadh na talmhan cho taithneamhach a's bhí súil
againn do bheith. Ní fheudóchadh aon-bheith dul amúghadhd-taobh
na d-tíolacaibh a bhronnfeas na Sasanaighe air mhuintir na h-
Éireann. Is amhlaidh tá siad dá mealladh le seacht g-ceud bliadhan.
Tig le oighridhe na talmhan iad a chrádhadh anois mar rinneadar a
riamh. Acht, mar dubhairt an sean-ráth, ‘Ní'l cobhairt a dhul a
dligheadh leis an diaghal a g-cuairt ifrionn’.

Ní'l aon cheo againn le déanadh anois acht congnamh a thabhairt
do chumann na talmhan. Cosnóchadh sé sin na daoine le chéile no go
m-beidh siad réidh le na g-cuid féin fhághail air ais, bhaint air ais. Tá
airm éifeachdach aig na daoine má gnigh siad úsáide dhíobhtha.
(Deireadh Fómhair 1881, 4)

Tá síothchán ann gach ball de’n domhan acht Éirinn amháin. Tá stáid
uadhbhásach; ní fad go m-beidh gach carcair innti lán de ‘braithibh’.
Tá timchioll cúig cheud a g-carcair ann Éirinn anois gan coir gan cúis;
níor bhriseadar aon dlíghe. Ná labhramois níos mó timchoill fóirneirt
Ruisia agus Turcia; tá Sacsainia níos measa ’ná ceachtar aca, is ni’l
modh dialtais 'na g-cumas anois a n-aghaidh na Sasannach damanta.
(Eanáir 1882, 26)

Is sé marbhadh na suadh Cabhendis agus an Búrcach an sgeul is
troime a thainic os comhair na n-daoinibh le suim d’aimsire. Tais-
beánann an marbhadh seo nach bh-fuil maighistreacht Gall taith-
<L 44>
neamhach le muintir na hEireann, ce b'é n duine a cuirfear d'a
fhriothóladh.

Gidh go bh-fuil fuath mór againn air aonduine a dheunóchadh
dochar d'chomhursa, caitfeamuid a rádh, leis an fhírinne a labhiart,
nach g-cuireann an t-ócáide seo aon doilghios croidhe orainn. Ní rabh
aon gnóthadh aig an g-Cabhendis a n-Éirinn, nó aig aon t-Sasanaigh
eile. Má fhanan na Sasanaigh ann a d-tír féin, lobhfaidh siad ann sul
do chuirfidh Éireannaidh móisiam orrtha mur nach bh-fanfaidh,
beidheadh an milleán orra féin ma chillfidh siad a g-cinn. (Bealtaine
1882, 74)

Facts and Fancies

If the power of dynamite were known in Cromwell’s time, it is
possible that the City of London would not contain to-day a popula-
tion close on four millions of people.

Dynamite is destined to play an important part in the destinies of
nations. The battle will not be with the strong, nor the race with the
fleet, in future. Any nationality, however weak, can defend itself now
from the strongest. For instance, suppose England commenced at her
old trick of butchering the Irish people, they (the Irish), with thirty
thousand men scattered all over England, could lay every city of it in
ruins, nay, they could get plenty of Englishmen to do it for the spoil. It
would be well for humanity that England would know this, because it
might prevent her from commiting acts of barbarity which, with a
sense of immunity from retribution, are always congenial to her. Yes,
it would be well for her to know that she is to-day at the mercy of
those whom she despoiled and oppressed, and also that patience has
her limits … (Deireadh Fómhair 1881, 4)

We have received over twenty communications during the month on
this subject. We now mean to answer all. The Gael was founded for
the purpose of teaching, and of agitating the cultivation of the Irish
language. It is no dynamite journal in the ordinary acceptance of the
term. We do not belong to any dynamite school or society. The Gael
discusses dynamite as a reality, like other journals … Heretofore
agitations looking to the freedom of Ireland in the open field, unarmed
and undisciplined against the power of England, seemed to us a very
mockery—an utter impossibility. Now that chemical science has
placed Irishmen in a different relation our sentiments have consider-
ably changed, and we believe that Irishmen can free themselves if they
have a mind to. But to do this, in our opinion, intelligence and un-
selfishness must guide and direct the operations. As the Visitor
observes, dynamite is the most terrible weapon ever discovered by
man; compared with it the power of England in an inland conflict is a
mere child’s play, because there is not a city in England but could be
razed to the ground without a single Irishman taking part in it. The
swell mob of London and Paris, and German hirelings could be
bought to do the work … As already observed these matters are
public property and The Gael makes no apology for adverting to
<L 44>
them, and it sees no difference between using dynamite and powder as
implements of warfare. Success only determines their relative values.
(Iúil 1883, 228)

‘Ach ní síltheagasc náisiúnta amháin a bhí á thabhairt ag an bhfear
eagair dá chuid léitheoirí—bhí teagasc sóisialta freisin:

We wish our readers would carefully read the following address of our
New Haven friend, Mr. O’Callaghan, and impress on all within their
reach the truths which it unfolds:

A Chairde,

Is deacair na sean fhocla a shárthugh  sé sin, 'dá fhad é ’n t-ól is tart
is deire dhó’. Ó thuit ar gceud athair i n-Gárdín Phárthais ní'l mí-adh
no mí-fhortún os comhair na n-daoine níos measa 'ná n-iomarca óil.

Marbhuigheann sé corp agus anam an té leanann de. An té dubhairt
‘bidheadh solas ann’, dubhairt mar an g-ceudna nach féidir leis an
druncéire dhul asteach trí gheatuidhe órdha na bh-flaitheas. Ins an
árus a m-bidheann biotáile is beoir, is gnáthach go m-bidheann buairt
’s brón ann. Do réir cómhaireamh oifigidhe léigheanta na tíre seo,
slugthar síos trí mhíle pótairidh gach bliadhain. Ní’l ach neimhní a d-
tinneas, galar no aicíd a g-cumas leis an léirsgrios a dheunas an t-ól.

Thuit an Róimh agus Caithir na Gréige trí chás an óil, agus do réir
taisbeánadh Mhic Aulaigh, tá tuitim gan éirighe roimh Shasana agus
sin go luath … Mo chúig cheud slán le laethibh m’óige, is iomadh
aistir fhada a rinne an t-Athair Maitiúgh 'san aimsir sin trí oileán glas
na h-Éireann, ag cómharlúgh na n-daoineadh druim a lámha a chur
le ól. Do réir mar mheallann duine dall dallachán eile, sin mar a
mheallas na pótairidhe a chéile; tuiteann mí-ádh agus mairg ortha
inseo, 's muna n-deunfidh siad aithrídhe lasaidh fearg Dé a n-anama
air fad na sioruigheachta … (Eanáir 1885, 446)

The Etiquette of the Pews—Having entered a pew, move along; do
not block up the end of a pew as if you did not intend to have
anybody else to enter it, or as if you were holding it for some special
friends. Do not rise to let others in, but move along and leave the pew
invitingly open, so that they will know they are welcome. If a pew
holding six has five already in it, do not file out in formal procession
to let one poor scared woman go to the other end, but move along and
let her sit at the end next the aisle. It is not necessary now for a
stalwart man to sit at the end, ready to rush out and kill Indians as
possibly it was once. (Lúnasa 1882, 115)

The Education of the English Aristocracy in the Thirteenth Century:
<L 46>
On the 24th of May, 1215, at Runnemede, the barons of England,
with Bishop De Langton, of Canterbury, at their head, presented a
petition to King John (Lackland), demanding Magna Charta. One-
third of the signatories signed with an X. So much for the enlighten-
ment of the nobility of England. (Deireadh Fómhair 1881, 4)

From the formulation of the Irish language into grammatical order by

ment in the world), in the year A.M. 1898, it did not cease to flourish,
until English vandalism, jealous of our nation’s fame for its sanctity
and learning, sought to destroy it … (Deireadh Fómhair 1881, 6)

<L 49>

LABOR

For some (to us) unaccountable cause the mass of the Irish people
seem to fancy that certain kinds of employment to earn a living are
more honorable than others. These erroneous ideas should be dis-
carded, as many suffer dire privations by indulging in them. The only
difference we see between any industrial pursuit is the degree of
remuneration which they yield. We throw out these timely hints,
because many of our country people coming to these friendly shores,
and who were reared in comparative opulence at home, suffer many
privations because they do not wish to engage in any occupation
which to them would seem undignified. This supercilious notion of
dignity permeates the social system in the old country to such a
degree that a shopkeeper would not be seen to sweep the sidewalk
before his store door, and those unable to pay help to do it would be
seen to watch the street to see that no one was in view to observe
them while they did it themselves … We have known young women
doing general housework in this city, whose ancestors were of the
most respectable families in the old country. Does the nature of their
employment detract from that respectability? Certainly not. True
dignity consists in an upright and cultivated mind. The possession of
wealth, or the wearing of fine clothes, does not confer dignity. True
dignity is inherent in the Celtic nature, and all that is required to make
it visible is a little polish … (Samhain 1881, 13)

The highest possible tone should be given to all our Gaelic enter-
tainments and extreme politeness and decorum observed in the class-
rooms. It does not necessarily follow that Irish entertainments should
be characterized by boisterousness and gom—acting. Educated and
self-respecting Irishmen well versed in the language and anxious to
promote the movement for its preservation do not care to be present
at entertainments not in accord with the advanced etiquette which is
born of the generally diffused knowledge of the present day . . .
(Feabhra 1886, 560)

Is é an áit a raibh an ‘polish’, an ‘tone’ ceart, an ‘advanced tone
and etiquette’ le fáil ag cruinnithe agus ag imeachtaí measúla na
‘Philo-Celts’:

Philo-Celts
The Philo Celtic society had their annual reunion and ball at Uris’
academy on April 19th, which was in all respects a complete success.
The following programme was gone through with great credit to the
participants:

Overture …  By the Orchestra
Opening Address … By the President
A Few Remarks in Irish … By the President
Song—“The Last Rose of Summer’
(in Irish)… Miss Nora T. Costello
Recitation-“Strike, no Matter How,
or Where … Master O’Shea
<L 50>
Song — ‘Departed Days’ … Mrs. McDonald
Recitation - ‘Mary Queen of Scots’  Doctor Leeming
Song — ‘Emmet’s Farewell’… Miss McGuirk
Song - ‘An Irishman’s Toast’… M. F. Costello
Song — ‘Killarney’… Miss Fox
Song - ‘Dear Little Shamrock’… Miss Sheridan
Duett … Messrs. Lewis and Maneely
Song — ‘Tara’s Hall’ … General Wm. DeLacy
Comic Recitation… Mr. Geo. T. Lewis

The hall was beautifully decorated, and the attendance was the
largest which the society has yet had. The music, which was under the
direction of Prof. Walters, was all that could be desired. The proceeds
of the entertainment will be applied to the purchase of a piano for the
use of the society … (Aibreán 1883, 200)

Philo-Celts

Let every one remember Wednesday, the 13th of August, at
Scheutzen Park, when the Brooklyn Philo-Celts hold their annual
picnic. That will be the one day in the year which shows that there
was ever such a thing as an Irish Nation. Here is where the sweet
language of the Gael will be heard in its pristine purity from the lips of
the thousands who scorn to barter the language of their sires for that
of the ‘slave’. Here may be seen the only legitimate heirs of those illus-
trious sages whose resplendent virtues and learning during the Dark
Ages of Europe, extracted from the surrounding nations the title for
their country of ‘The Island of Saints and Scholars’. Here may be
seen the pioneers of a regenerated Ireland, calm collected, with the
impress of self-reliance of their foreheads and independent defiance in
their demeanour, in a word, there may be seen men and women
standing to their full height, conscious of inheriting from a noble
ancestry those principles of virtue and morality characteristic of their
nation and which centuries of oppression and tyranny and the con-
tiguous Sodomatic abominations of England could not pervert. The
contemplation of these truths should enkindle a glow of pride in the
heart of every Irishman. He should remember that the word Irish was
a synonym with all that is noble in human nature until the tares of
Anglo-Saxon beastiality got intermixed with his race. This is no mere
rhetoric, but is a fact supported and demonstrated by ancient and
modern—aye, and very modern—witnesses! Then you Irishmen and
women who desire to preserve and perpetuate those noble traits of
your ancestry, go to your Irish picnic on the 13th and enjoy a day of
amusement in company with your fellow-countrymen, embued with
the same sentiments … (Iúil 1884, 380)

Is léir cé leis a _bhfuilimid ag plé san iris, cén aicme Ghael-
Mheiriceánach atá á casadh orainn: an dream a raibh an chéad
chúpla runga ar an slí aníos bainte amach acu agus a bhí ag iarraidh
an chéad runga eile a bhaint amach chomh tapaidh agus a b'fhéidir.
Is maith a thuigeadar féin an méid sin:

<L 51>
Boston, Feb 21st, — Mayor Grace, of New York, lectured to night in
the Boston Theatre to over 4,000 people on behalf of the Carney
Hospital. His subject was, ‘The Irish in America’. Mayor O’Brien
presided and on the platform were Lieutenant Governor Ames, Ex-
Governor Rice and other prominent men … The above is one of the
most remarkable as well as one of the most important incidents
recorded in American history as far as the Irish element is concerned.
Remarkable because it is within the memory of men not yet old when
the City of Boston did not embrace within her limits a single Irish
Catholic householder, yet, on the 21. of Feb. 1886 the Irish Catholic
Mayor of that city, in her leading theatre, presented to an audience of
over four thousand people, of the elite of the city and state, the Irish
Catholic mayor of New York City - the metropolis of the Western
World - as the lecturer of the evening. The contemplation of such a
state of things fifty years ago would be treated as a romance. The
Irishman has been bruised, maimed, scarified, but his recuperative
powers seem to border on the miraculous—the plaster is peeling off
his wounds and requires but slight nourishment to restore him to a
state of perfect convalescence … (Feabhra 1886, 566)

It can hardly be conceived what injury this English cry of “ignorant
Irish’ entails on Irish-Americans and what pertains to them. They lose
in their social standing; and Catholicism and Irishism being synono-
mous terms with Americans, they actually believe that the Irish are
Catholic because of their ‘ignorance’. Now the Irish were Catholic
when they were the instructors of Europe, and the proof of that fact
should find its way into every Catholic school-book and every
Catholic newspaper in the land … (Iúil 1890, 975)
<L 52>
We hear of late much said of the rapid increase of Catholicity in the
United States.—Not at all—only the normal increase; but the Gaelic
Movement has so strengthened the backbone in Irishmen that they no
longer deny their country of their religion, and the so-called Catholic
increase is proportionate with its operated extent … (Márta' 1893,
259)

The glorious festival of our Patron Saint was celebrated here in this
home, in a very pious and christianlike manner. Three Catholic
priests came here from Daytonn, at 2 pm on Sunday afternoon, and
assisted the Rev. Dr. C. Kemper to hear the confessions of the old
veterans, and on St. Patrick's day nearly all the Catholics — German
as well as Irish, received Holy Communion, and the ‘Hibernian
Society’ of the home went to the altar in a body, and received Holy
Communion; it was very beautiful and edifying … The Rev. Fr.
Cusack delivered the sermon — it was more of a lecture than a
sermon - Subject: ‘The Government aided Proselytizing Schools’ and
the “Famine in Ireland’. The subject was ably and well handled, and it
caused the hearers to shed tears abundantly. When the Government
Proselytizers came to the starving multitudes and said to
them,—Here change your religion and come with us, and you shall
have plenty to eat and wear’— but our people spurned them and died
in thousands on the road-side and in their cabins. Martyrs for the
Holy Faith that St. Patrick so firmly planted in their Christian hearts,
and “the blood of the martyrs is the seed of the church’. And he said
the sufferings and martyrdom of so many Christians and their blood
have cried out loudly to Heaven for vengeance and the even handed
justice of God requires him to do justice to Ireland. “Revenge is mine
and I will repay’, says the Lord. He said if the Irish people would be
true to their religion and to their God there seemed now to be a bright
prospect before them and their deliverance was near at hand. (Meán
Fómhair 1894, 340)

Múinteoir ba ea an Lógánach i dtús ama ach d’éirigh sé sa saol de
réir a chéile agus le blianta beaga anuas is le hárachas, le heastát
réadach agus le hamhantraíocht talún a bhíodh sé ag plé. Ba
ghníomhaire eastát, ba nótaire poiblí agus ba choimisinéar mionnaí
ina theanntasan é agus is beag uimhir den iris nach mbíodh fógra gnó
aige féin ann. Obair dheonach, ní miste a rá, ba ea an iriseoireacht
agus obair na Gaeilge agus is dóichí gurbh é a phóca féin a bhí thíos
leis ach ba theann an póca é agus níor cheart naomh ar fad a
<L 55>
dhéanamh de. Sa bhliain 1886 chuir sé féin faoi bhráid léitheoirí na
hirise scéim mhór a bhí aige: comhlacht a bhunú chun breis agus
600,000 acra talún a cheannach saor ar $2 an t-acra ó chomhlacht
eile a bhí bancbhriste agus an talamh a dhíol arís ar $5 an t-acra do
na mílte Éireannach a bhí caite i dteannta a chéile i mbailte plúchta an
chósta thoir. Bheadh dhá thoradh fhónta ar an scéim seo, dar
leis—chabhródh sé leis na hainniseoirí a bhí díreach tagtha ón tsean-
tir agus thabharfadh sé caoi don dream a bhí ann rompu airgead bog
a dhéanamh orthu:

The Company proposes to place any honest man willing to work on a
100 acre farm of good land, build him a house, sink him a well, pro-
vide him with farming implements, etc., to enable him to raise his first
crop, give him the land at $5 an acre, 100 acres for $500, on very
easy instalments, and after paying these $500 the land is his own for
ever. This is the greatest chance ever offered to labor … It has been
said that up to this time the lot of the Irishman has been cast among
“the hewers of wood and drawers of water’— and this has been so.
Now it is time for him to make a little change, and if Irishmen be true
to each other they can become, with very little exertion, not only
independent but the bone and sinew of the land —  ‘their country’s
pride’… We have rich Irishmen in this country who are daily
looking after schemes to profitably invest their capital. Now, all the
spare capital among Irishmen can be profitably invested in the
colonization of the millions of acres of fine land lying idle in the States
with their poorer countrymen. Buying land at $2 or $3 an acre in
large quantities and selling it to their poor countrymen on terms
which would enable them in a short time to become their own land-
lords, and at the same time secure a profitable investment for them-
selves, is an undertaking in which every Irishman, having the means,
should with alacrity take part … It will take a large capital to do this
thoroughly, but this capital will make a tenfold return both in cash
and in the consciousness that the capitalist has done that which
Wisdom expected of wealth when it propounded the interrogatory:
Thug mé maoin díbh, agus creud a rinne sibh leis? … (Eanáir 1886,
5 18)

Now, there is no doubt but this company will make money; but if a
hardworking, struggling mechanic or laborer, say after five or ten
years, is enabled to become the owner of a 100 acre farm of good
land by the Company’s aid, is he not, as well as the company, the
gainer to the amount of value of his farm? … Nature intended the
Irish for an agricultural people. They inhabit one of the most fertile
sections and possess one of the most genial climates in the world.
Injustice and bad government have prevented them from improving
their opportunities. But there is no reason why a New Ireland should
not rise west of the Mississippi, where the starving kerne of
Connaught may exchange his present miserable existence for that of a
happy and prosperous farmer … (Feabhra 1886, 556)
<L 56>
Ní bheadh sé féarálta againne a rá cén ceann den dá thoradh sin is
mó a bhí in aigne lucht na scéime ach is deacair gan aigne agus
mianach agus modh oibre Tamanny Hall a fheiscint laistiar di. Níor
éirigh leis an scéim, ní nach iontach; ní raibh aon taithí ag formhór na
n-ainniseoirí a tháinig anall ó Éirinn ar thalamh dá gcuid féin a
shealbhú ná a shaothrú — sclábhaíocht lae do mháistir nó
d’athmháistir b’in é a dtaithísean ar fheirmeoireacht (eisceacht ba ea
Pádraig Phiarais Cúndún). Ina theanntasan b’é comhairle a sagart
dóibh fanacht i dteannta a chéile sna bailte ar chóngar an aifrinn.

Cailleadh an Lógánach sa bhliain 1899 agus chuaigh An Gaodhal
féin in éag sa bhliain 1904 ach cuimhne bhuan is ea an iris, anois, ní
amháin ar an Lógánach agus ar a chuid saothair ach ar imeachtaí
agus ar aigne an Ghael-Mheiriceánaigh i mblianta deireanacha an
chéid seo caite.

<L 57>

CEOL NA hÉIREANN AGUS AMHRÁIN NA nDAOINE SNA
STÁIT AONTAITHE

W. H. A. WILLIAMS

Cad a bhaineann do cheol na ndaoine nuair a théann na daoine
féin thar lear? Is é is éadroime sa bhagáiste, an té is boichte tig leis lán
a chinn de a iompar, gan cánacha custaim ná mál a íoc. Agus níl sé
chomh leochaileach le píosaí eile den bhagáiste sin: na seanscéalta, an
teanga féin ina reictear iad, is minic nach dtagann siad slán an tan
aistríonn ó ascaill an ghleanna go deatach na cathrach. Dlúth agus
inneach sa chultúr na daoine agus an taobh tíre as a síolraíonn siad.
Gearr snáth amháin agus titfidh an mianach go léir ina ghiobal.

An ceol traidisiúnta a aistrítear as an tír le himirce na gceoltóirí, tig
leis meascadh le ceol na tíre is aoi dóibh, tig leis maireachtáil gan
meascadh taobh istigh den chultúr agus den chine inar thaistil sé, nó
tig leis bás a fháil diaidh ar ndiaidh. Mar sin, uimhreacha a haon, a
dó agus a trí, a tharla do cheol na hÉireann sna Stáit Aontaithe. Bhí
tionchar láidir aige (le ceolta eile) ar fhorbairt ceoil tíre na Stát, ar an
bpopcheol féin; mhair sé tamall taobh istigh de ghetto na nGael-
Mheiriceánach; agus ó aimsir an dara cogadh domhanda i leith, tá sé
ag dul i laige, go dtí nach bhfuil ann anois ach an dé deiridh. Ach,
fá'n, ní críoch brónach ar fad a bheidh leis an scéal. Le blianta beaga
anuas, tá suim nua á mhúscailt sa cheol seo, agus le háiseanna nua-
aimsire, an taifeadadh agus an teilifís, gan trácht ar an eitleán a
thugann grúpaí oilte proifisiúnta ar chuairt go dtí na Stáit, tá borradh
nua le tabhairt faoi deara sa seinm féin.

Scéal fada casta scéal seo ceoil na hÉireann sna Stáit, agus, rud is
iontach, is beag atá scríofa faoi go dtí seo. Fanaimis le dhá cheist, cad
é an tionchar a bhí ag an gceol seo ar cheol na Stát, agus conas mar a
mhair sé? Cloífimid san alt seo le scéal na n-amhrán; ar ocáid eile,
b’fhéidir, beidh deis againn tabhairt faoin gceol uirlise.

Glactar leis go hiondúil gur meascán ceol tíre na Stát de dhá
thraidisiún, ceann amháin a tháinig ón Eoraip, is é sin le rá go mór
mór ó Shasana, ó Albain agus ó Éirinn, agus an ceann eile ón Aifric,
lena nósanna chomhcheoil agus rithime, stíl urlabhraíochta agus stíl
déanta amhrán, rud a thug na sclábhaithe dubha leo. Dlúth agus
inneach arís an dá thraidisiún seo agus nuair a thugann muid faoi
snáth na hÉireann a aithint, tá mórshaothar romhainn.

Meiriceánaigh is mó, ar ndóigh, a dhein staidéar ar a gceol féin,
agus go minic bhí siad sásta an téarma “Breataineach’ a úsáid chun
foinse foinn a * ainmniú, agus go minic freisin b’ionann leo
“Breataineach’ agus ‘Sasanach’. Tuigimis dóibh, mar sin féin: mar a
dúirt L. Lloyd (Folksong in England), tá go leor fonn, &rl., atá le fáil
ar an dá thaobh de Mhuir Mheann. ‘Such tunes incline to sound

<L 58>
English in England, Irish in Ireland and Scots in the mouth of a
Lothian harvester … ’ (lch 76). Má thagann muid ar an bhfonn
chéanna i Kentucky, fuaim Mheiriceánach a bheidh leis.

Bíodh sin mar atá, bhí an tionchar láidir sin ag an gceol as Éirinn
ar cheol na Stát. Mheas údar amháin (Bruno Nettl, An introduction
to folk music in the United States) go bhfuil thart ar dhá chéad
bailéidí sráide ‘Breataineacha’ i measc an phobail sna Stáit. ‘A
considerable number, perhaps as much as one third … are of Irish
origin’ (lch 7). Léirigh Lloyd gur chuaigh na foinn Éireannacha go
láidir i bhfeidhm ar amhráin na ndaoine i Sasana san ochtú haois
déag agus gur fhréamhaigh stileanna agus saghsanna amhrán as seo
a bhí dúchasach amach ansin i go leor aird i Sasana (lch 210).

Beidh méirín treoir le fail againn san leagan amach a bhí ar theacht
na nÉireannach go tuaisceart Mheiriceá; luífeadh sé le réasún, más
ea, gur as Ultaibh a tháinig an chéad bhrú ceolmhar as Éirinn. Faoi
1776 bhí ceathrú milliún Ultach sna Trí Choilíneacht Déag. Céad
mile eile idir sin agus 1815. Preisbitéirigh a bhformhór, a shíolraigh
uathu-san as deisceart Alban a lonnaigh i dtuaisceart Éireann sa
seachtú haois déag. B'iad an dream ba líonmhaire, ach amháin na
Sasanaigh féin, i measc lucht áitribh Mheiriceá. ‘Irish’ a tugadh orthu
ar dtús, ach faoi lár an naoú haois déag ba nos ‘Scots-Irish’ no
‘Scotch’ a thabhairt orthu chun dealaithe eatarthu-san agus na
hÉireannaigh Chaitliceacha a bhí ag brú isteach mar thonn tuile faoin
am sin. Ceapann E. Estynn Evans go raibh cultúr suntasach da gcuid
féin ag na Preisbitéirigh Ultacha seo: ‘ their major lasting
contribution to the American scene was their broad impact on the
American landscape and way of life’. (Essays in Scotch-Irish history,
lch 76). Cúrsaí tithíochta, leagan-amach feirme agus baile, agus a
leithéid siúd atá i gceist aige, ach is cosúil gurb é an ceol an
tabhartas is mó agus is buaine a bhronn siad ar na Stáit. Is cuma faoi
chúrsaí creidimh agus cine, ar shlí; i gcúrsaí ceoil dob Ultaigh iad
agus thugadar leo an meascán sin de foinn Éireannacha agus
Albanacha atá fós le clos i gcuid mhaith de thuaisceart Éireann.

De bharr cúrsaí áirithe sóisialta agus eacnamaíochta, lonnaigh na
hUltaigh seo ar an line teorainn, an ‘frontier’, i New England agus i
bPennsylvania i dtús báire, ag sileadh ar aghaidh uaidh sin ó dheas trí
ghleannta agus sléibhte Appalachia chomh fada le Virginia agus
Carolina. Ba iad, is é sin le rá, a chéadlonnaigh i sléibhte an deiscirt
agus is sa cheantar sin is treise agus is buaine a d'fhás traidisiún an
cheoil tire. Mar atá ráite ag W. H. Tallmadge, tá íoróin áirithe le
tabhairt faoi deara — nuair a chuaigh Cecil Sharpe isteach in
Appalachia ar thóir amhrán ‘Sasanacha’ is minic ag pie le sliocht
Ultach a bhí sé. Measann Tallmadge go raibh nós déanta agus canta
amhrán go láidir i measc na nUltach seo: fiú amháin nuair nárbh iad
ba líonmhaire, bhí tionchar as miosúr ag an gceol a bhí acu. Áit ar
<L 59>
bith inar lonnaigh siad, agus iad scartha go maith ó aicmí eile, tá na
bailéidí le fail go beo bríomhar.

Mar sin féin, ní furasta i gcónaí an dúil ‘Scotch-Irish’ seo a aithint i
ndeisceart na Stát. Is beag amhrán a dtagtar air a bhfuil an dúchas
Éireannach go suntasach ann. Samplaí: The Irish girl (The
Blackwater side, amanna, amanna eile I’m a poor stranger and far
from my own, leagan Joyce, amanna meascán de véarsaí fánacha a
sníomhtar amhrán Meiriceánach as, Handsome Molly), Erin's green
shore (leagan de ag lucht an deiscirt i gCogadh na Stat faoin dteideal
Dixie's green shore), Brennan on the moor, The Boston burglar.

Ní haon ionadh nár fhan na hamhráin a dhein trácht ar chúrsaí na
hÉireann go pointeálta beo i dtraidisiún Appalachia. Ach nár mhaith
linn a fhios a bheith againn cé mhéid de na hamhráin sin a luaitear an
téarma ‘Breataineach’ leo a tháinig leis na himirceoirí Ultacha agus
atá, dá bhrí sin, ina leaganacha Éireannacha d’amhráin Sasanacha
nó ina n-amhráin dúchasacha Éireannacha. Dhein Norman Casden
bailiúchán de bhailéidí a chantar i stát Nua-Eabhrac agus chreid sé
go raibh sé ‘soiléir’ gur leagan Éireannach a bhí sa leagan a fuair sé
de Captain Widderburn's courtship. Cúpla bliain ó shin thug D. K.
Wilgus léacht do Chumann Cheol-Tíre Éireann inar léirigh sé gur as
fréamh Éireannach a d’fhás Rose Connolly, bailéad Meiriceánach a
chantar fós go minic.

Fiú don cheol-eolaí oilte, is ceist chasta ceist seo fréamhacha na
bhfonn a aithint. Agus is beag scoláire Meiriceánach a chuaigh i
muinín na mbailiúchán Éireannacha. Eisceacht George Pullen
Jackson, fear a chaith a shaol ag déanamh staidéir ar amhráin diaga
dheisceart na stát. Timpeall dhá chéad caoga atá aige i Spiritual
folksongs of early America agus tugaim faoi dear a gur luaigh sé
fréamh Éireannach don cheathrú cuid díobh siúd a mheas sé a tháinig
ón Eoraip. Codán, creidim, atá ró-íseal. Mheas sé, mar shampla, go
raibh fréamh Gearmánach ag an iomann Greenfields (rogha Lincoln i
measc na n-iomann go léir) agus is follas domsa gur Rosin the bow ba
bhun leis. Níor thug sé faoi deara ach oiread an chosúlacht atá idir
The lonesome valley agus suantraí ag Petrie (Ancient music of Ireland
ii, lch 38). Go bhfios dom, níor tugadh an coibhneas sin faoi deara go
dtí seo. Is mar fhonn leis an amhrán diaga Dia do bheatha is fearr atá
aithne ar an bhfonn seo in Éirinn inniu.

Ni raibh ach dhá bhailiúchán Éireannacha á n-úsáid ag Jackson
(Joyce, Irish music and song, 1901, agus eagrán Stanford de Petrie).
Dá mbeadh tuilleadh, seans gur mó dá réir sin a bheadh lion na
bhfonn Éireannacha aige. Thuig sé go maith mar sin féin an dúil
Éireannach a bhí in iomainn an deiscirt. Thug sé faoi deara gur
daoine de shliocht Éireannach agus Gearmánach is mó a chleacht an
córas ‘fa-so-la’ (córas simplí scríofa agus léamh an cheoil). Ach ba
chonstaic, dar leis, an Ghearmáinis agus b’iad na ‘Scotch-Irish’ dá
<L 60>
bhrí sin a thug an cor Ceilteach do cheol an cheantair.

Tá níos lú den chor Éireannach ar an rannóg sin de na hamhráin a
dtugann Jackson ‘revival spirituals’ orthu. Bhí dlúthcheangal idir na
hamhráin sin ag na Baistigh agus na Meitidistigh agus amhráin
spioradálta an chine ghoirm, agus dul fíorMheiriceánach orthu dá réir
sin. Ach b’aisteach leis na hÉireannaigh Chaitliceacha iad. Tá mír
ghreannmhar i The cross and shamrock or how to defend the faith
(‘sagart misnéireach’ a scríobh), tráchtas deabhóideach Caitliceach i
bhfoirm úrscéil. Aor ar na hamhráin seo an mhír sin. Mealltar beirt
bhuachaill óg, baill den chlann úd atá tar éis teacht go Meiriceá is
ábhar don scéal, mealltar iad chuig cruinniú ag na Meitidistigh.
Cuirtear ceist orthu ar ball cad a chuala siad. Chan na mná, deir siad,
‘Oh, for a man, oh for a man, oh for a mansion in the skies’, agus
chan na fir ‘Send down sal, send down sal, send down salvation to
our souls’. Scairt gáirí, ar ndóigh, agus iarrtar ar na buachaillí rud
éigin a mhúin na Bráithre dóibh a chanadh. Lámh ar láimh a chéile,
canann siad Ave Maria.

In 1853 a foilsíodh an leabhar seo, i mBaile Átha Cliath. Faoin am
sin, bhí imirce na gCaitliceach ina sruth. Ní fhéadfaí cloí a thuilleadh
leis an nós a chleachtaítí san ochtú haois déag agus ‘Éireannaigh’ a
thabhairt orthu go léir. Bhí an chonspóid creidimh Ian chomh
nimhneach sna Stáit, in áiteanna, agus a bhí in Éirinn féin.

Siad na himirceoirí nua seo a thugann go dtí pointe eile muid i stair
cheoil na hÉireann sna Stáit. Feirmeoirí a bhí sa chuid ba mhó de na
hUltaigh Phreisbitéireacha, daoine a lonnaigh ar an ‘Frontier’, mar a
dúirt muid. Ba bheag Ultach eile a tháinig chun tacaíocht a thabhairt
dóibh agus mheasc a gcuid ceoil faoi dheireadh leis an dtimpeallacht
agus tháinig crot ceart Meiriceánach air.

Is sna cathracha is mó a lonnaigh na himirceoirí nua (na
Caitlicigh, i gcoitinne). Bhunaigh siad fo-chultúr ceoil dá gcuid féin
sna cathracha. Dá réir sin is lú an tionchar a bhí acu ar an gceol tire
Meiriceánach. Mar sin féin, bhí tionchar áirithe ann.

Chuaigh siad ag lorg oibre, ag tochailt canálacha agus bóithre
iarainn, ag leagadh crann i dtuaisceart tire, ag mianadóireacht, ag
buachailleacht bó. Agus d’fhág siad lorg a gceoil ina ndiaidh. D'fhan
siad taobh amuigh den deisceart, mar a chonaic muid, agus tá an lorg
sin le sonrú sa tuaisceart agus san iarthar.

Caithfidh gur Gaeilgeoirí cuid mhaith díobh agus caithfidh go
raibh amhráin Ghaeilge acu. Mar a tharla i gcás na nGearmánach
(féach thuas), is minic gur bac an teanga iasachta ar iontráil na n-
amhrán i dtraidisiún an bhéaloideasa. Nil oiread agus amhrán
Gaeilge amháin le fail sna bailiúcháin mhóra Mheiriceánacha, cé go
bhfuil corrthagairt ag na heagarthóirí do na leithéid a bheith á

Tá roint samplaí den véarsa Béarla agus an curfá Gaeilge leis.
marc 2-12-2019
<L 61>
Shule Arune an ceann is coitianta. Faoin teideal Johnny has gone for
a soldier is minicí a thagtar air agus bhí sé furasta é a chur in oiriúint
do chúrsaí na Réabhlóide. Dar le Alan Lomax, úsáideadh an fonn
freisin mar amhrán mara, mar amhrán súgradh do pháistí, mar
amhrán ag lucht foraoiseachta agus mar amhrán sollúnta ag na
Cherokee, fiú amháin! I ngach foirm, ámh, ba luath a thit an curfá
Gaeilge ina ghliogar. Leagan as Missouri:

Shule, shule shule-amac-a-me
Shule-mac-a-rac-stack, Sally Bobby Lee (bis)
Come bibble-ue-a-boose — said Lora.

Mhair iarracht éigin den Ghaeilge slán, ámh. I stat Maine, bhí
leagan den Gay wedding agus curfá Gaeilge leis. (Eckstrom agus
Smyth, Minstrelsy of Maine, lch 170).

Amhrán neamhchoitianta ar fad Oh my poor Drimendroo. Tá
leagan de faoin teideal sin i mbailiúchán na mbailéad singil sa
Leabharlann Náisiúnta i mBaile Átha Cliath, amhrán faoin sean-
fhear nach raibh ach an t-aon bhó aige. Nil curfá leis. Ach i
mbailiúchán na Roinne Béaloideasa, sa Choláiste Ollscoile, Baile
Átha Cliath (Is 134, lgh 380-1) tá leagan de ina bhfuil véarsaí Béarla
agus curfá Gaeilge. Ni thugtar fonn le ceachtar den dá leagan seo ach
is cosúil go bhfuil na véarsaí gaolmhar lena chéile. Nil an
t-amhrán le fail in aon cheann de na bailiúcháin clóbhuailte sna Stáit
ach chuir an t-amhránaí clúiteach Huddie Ledbetter (‘Leadbelly’)
leagan de ar cheirnín. Giotaí aige as an mbailéad singil agus as an
leagan ón mbéaloideas. Curfá aisteach freisin: Oh, oh, which is
sweeter than down. Nil brí ar bith leis sa chomhthéacs agus b’fhéidir
gur leagan truaillithe den Ghaeilge é. Thóg na Weavers an t-aon line
sin amháin, dhein 'Kisses sweeter than wine’ as agus chum amhrán
dá gcuid féin ar fhonn Leadbelly.

B'iad na bailéidí sráide, a cuireadh i gcló (i ndroch-chló, go ró-
mhinic) mar bhailéidí singil, ba mhó as Éirinn a chuaigh i bhfeidhm ar
an gceol tire Meiriceánach, an gné de atá faoi chaibidil againn anois.
Foinn simplí a bhí leo, córas rime soiléir, agus b’fhurasta iad a úsáid
mar eiseamláir ag an té ar theastaigh uaidh a amhrán féin a chumadh.
Measann Lloyd gur foirm an ‘come-all-ye’ seo a d’fhás sna cathracha
(Baile Átha Cliath?) san ochtú haois déag. Thug lucht na himirce leo
é go Sasana, go hAlbain, go dtí an Astráil agus, ar ndóigh, go dtí
Meiriceá. Na sclábhaithe Éireannacha a scaip é i measc na
mianadóirí agus na n-aicmí saothair eile, lucht leagtha na gcrann go
háirithe. ‘In the logging camp, the hegemony in song belonged to the
Irish … the Irish street song was the pattern upon which a liberal
portion of the shanty songs were made’. (Malcolm Laws, jr., Native
American balladry, 1950, lch 107). Mheas Phillips Barry, duine den
bheagán bailitheoirí Meiriceánacha a raibh tuiscint acu do cheol na

<L 62>
hÉireann, gur léirigh traidisiún na n-amhrán in oirthear Maine
(ceantar foraoiseachta): ‘the Irish element … is much greater than it
is generally supposed to be … it has done more than its share in
preserving the old songs.’  Arís ‘it is from singers with some Irish
blood that we have obtained the most songs’ (Tallmadge, lch 273).

Ni ionann agus an deisceart, sa tuaisceart táthar tar éis roinnt
mhaith amhrán Éireannach, bailéidí sráide, a bhailiú. The croppy
boy, Rinadine, Willy Taylor, The battle of the Boyne, Morrissey and
the Russian sailor, The green mossy banks of the Lee, agus go leor
eile. Ar uaire is féidir teacht sna Stáit ar amhrán Éireannach atá
neamhchoitianta go leor in Éirinn féin. Sa bhliain 1911, i Missouri,
thángthas ar leagan de Jimmy Murphy, nó Joe Jimmy Murphy (a
crochadh ‘not for sheep stealin’ but for courtin’ a pretty maid and her
name was Kate Whelan,’). Taobh amuigh de chúpla mionphointe
agus véarsa breise, is é an t-amhrán céanna é a chloistear uaireanta in
Éirinn. Tá curfá Missouri níos aistí fós ná curfá na hÉireann.

Then rall-a-bony lass now
From the east ot Dan Pathrow's
To entice poor Jimmy Murphy
From the green mossy banks of the
Jam-spooderfadler-ram-jamfa-defa-da-riddle-die-do-too-yi-ya.

Tá amhráin eile ann go mb’fhéidir gur as Éirinn a shíolraigh siad
ach go bhfuil an dul Gael-Mheiriceánach orthu. Ní mór an treoir ar
an ábhar na teidil: Dublin Bay, Steve O’Donnell’s wake (max ar éirigh
gach éinne ólta ar ndóigh), I left mother and Ireland because we were
poor, When Pat Malone forgot that he was dead (‘until he smelt the
whiskey at the wake’), Jack Haggarty (amhrán foraoiseachta
coitianta), Pat O’Brien (bailéad faoi dhúnmharú), agus mar sin de.

Maoithneachas, d’fhéadfá a rá: ‘Take them to my brother, for I
have no other, and they are the shamrocks for his dear old mother’s
grave’. Ba ábhar tráchtála an maoithneachas seo go luath, a bhí
dírithe ar mhargadh i measc na nGael-Mheiriceánach. Is ceart a rá
mar sin féin go raibh era croí fírinneach á fhulaingt ag an lucht imirce
agus gurb é a bhí mar bhun leis na hamhráin seo bheith chomh
maoithneach agus a bhí siad.

Go leor de na hamhráin seo, is i dtraidisiún grinn an Éireannaigh
stáitse atá siad. Is minic nár dhein siad ach fiúntas na nGael chun
troda nó chun oil a cheiliúradh agus is minic nach raibh iontu ach an
Gaelachas i gcéin á cheiliúradh, tri liosta ainmneacha agus sloinnte a
thabhairt. Sampla maith Naming the boy, curtha ar fail ag Patrick
McKenna don United Mine Workers’ Journal, 1910. (Ar fail in Coal
dust on the fiddle, le George Korson.)
<L 63>
I've invited the Brady's, the O'Grady's,
And O’Brien, Gilhooly and Dennis McCoy
Pat McConnell and Fagin, Dan Hagin and Flynn —  
Will be there when I’m naming the boy.

Ní greann amháin atá iontu. Ba chrua an cás ina raibh na
sclábhaithe seo, mianadóirí, muilleoirí sna monarchana cruach — pá
íseal, uaireanta fada baolacha. Cé go bhfuil greann áirithe in Pat
works on the railroad nó Drill ye terriers drill nó No Irish need
apply, is greann searbh é agus faobhar leis. I gceantar an ghuail i
bPennsylvania chan siad faoi Mickey McGinn (‘everyone said he was
ugly as sin’) a shábháil caoga dá chomrádaithe ón mbás.

On naoú haois déag i leith bhí Éireannaigh agus Gael-
Mheiriceánaigh gníomhach i ngluaiseacht na gceardchumann. Bhí
Mary Harris as Corcaigh, Mother Jones mar a thugtaí uirthi, ina
heagraitheoir díograiseach sna mianraí guail agus sna muilte cruach ó
1870 go dtí 1919, tráth a ghlac sí páirt agus i in aois a hochtó naoi, i
stailc mhór na cruach. Bhí Éireannaigh chun tosaigh sna Wobblies
agus da bhrí sin tá go leor amhrán agóide agus amhrán ceard-
chumainn ann a bhfuil séala na hÉireann orthu.

Mar a chonaic muid ó chianaibh, tá iarracht den halla ceoil ag
baint le cuid mhaith de na hamhráin Gheal-Mheiriceánacha, agus is
fiú dá bhrí sin féachaint tamaillín ar an méid a chuir Éireannaigh ar
fail do ‘phopcheol’ na Stat, ni ionann agus an ceol tire. Ní gá a rá go
raibh eolas maith ar amhráin Thomas Moore. Rud níos suimiúla,
b’fhéidir, ba Ghael-Mheiriceánaigh cuid mhaith de bhunaitheoirí an
nós ‘black-face’. (Daoine geala ag cur i gcéill go mba daoine dubha
iad, mar abhar grinn.). Dan Emmett, Joel Sweeney (a mhaígh, go
falsa, gurbh é a chéadcheap an banjo cúig sreang). Ach ni raibh lucht
an aghaidh dhuibh, na ‘nigger minstrels’, fabhrach do na Caitlicigh;
mar aon le haor ar a gcomhshaoránaigh daite, bhíodh aor acu ar na
hÉireannaigh. Faoi dheireadh, chuaigh Gael-Mheiriceánaigh ar
aghaidh iad féin ar ardán na ‘minstrels’ — Dan Bryant, George
Christy, Matt Campbell, John Collins. Bhí clú faoi leith ar Edward
Harrigan. Thosaigh sé mar ‘aghaidh dhubh’, ach amach ansin, i
gcomhar le ‘Tony Hart’, (Anthony Cannon) scríobh sé agus léirigh sé
sraith drámaí ina raibh Dan Mulligan mar phríomhcharachtar,
drama! a chuir dealramh níos taitneamhaí den Ghael-Mheiriceánach
os comhair an phobail. Mar thoradh ar an rath a bhí orthu, b’éigin do
na ‘minstrels’ aghaidh níos tuisceanaí a thabhairt ar na hÉireannaigh,
agus bhí roinnt amhrán acu a chain an díspeagadh a deineadh ar na
comhshaoránaigh geala seo.

Bhí lucht féachana Éireannach sna cathracha agus theastaigh
drama! agus amhráin fúthu féin uathu. Ghaibh go leor Éireannach le
haisteoireacht agus le cumadóireacht. Deir Deac Martin ina leabhar
ar phopcheol Mheiriceá: ‘in the song record, the Irish had more
<L 64>
influence than any other nationality until recent years.’ Admhaíonn
sé, ámh, gurbh iad lucht Tin Pan Alley, dála William Jerome agus
Jean Schwartz, cumadóirí My Irish rose, a chuir go leor de na
hamhráin ‘Éireannacha’ ar fail.

Toise gur éirigh na hÉireannaigh (a bhí, tar éis an tsaoil, sna Stáit
roimh Polannaigh, Iodáiligh, Slavaigh), toise gur éirigh siad chomh
hardghlórach sa tírghrá Mheiriceánach a nocht siad, deir Martin go
raibh ‘a song of Irish flavour … almost as effective as waving an
American flag.’ Tugann sé liosta de chaoga de na hamhráin seo a
bhfuil bias Éireannach leo. Tá go leor den ‘Blarney’ ina measc. All
the Irish do the tango, Has anybody here seen Kelly? Ireland must be
heaven (for my mother came from there), Mother Machree, Danny
Boy. I measc cumadóirí Gael-Mheiriceánacha an phopcheoil áirítear
Edward Harrigan, Stephen Foster, Henry McCarthy, Charles
Carroll Sawyer, K. K. O’Donnell, an tAth. Abram Ryan, Rose
Cary, Patrick Sarsfield Gilmore agus, ar ndóigh, Victor Herbert.

Agus ná ligimis i ndearmad an cumadóir Francis Scott Key, an té
a chum an t-amhrán úd is minicí a seinntear sna Stáit, cé go
mb’fhéidir nach é an t-amhrán é is fearr le muintir na tire seo, an Star
spangled banner.

<L 65>

NÓTAÍ AR GHLUAISEACHT NA GAEILGE
I MEIRICEÁ, 1872-1891

LIAM Ó DOCHARTAIGH

Ar ócáid oscailte an chéad chraobh de bhanc Éireannach ar mhór-
roinn Mheiriceá i mí Eanáir 1978, scríobh Ambasadóir na Stát
Aontaithe in Éirinn, William V. Shannon, alt gearr in iris an bhainc
ar na hÉireannaigh sna Stáit Aontaithe. I measc a raibh le rá ag an
Ambasadóir, bhí an méid seo faoi chathair Nua-Eabhrac sa bhliain
1880:

By 1880, New York was a city of 1,200,000 people. Of these
198,000 had been born in Ireland. Another 207,000 had a father
born in Ireland. When to these numbers were added those of third and
fourth-generation Irish ancestry, it is probable that the Irish con-
stituted a majority of New York’s population. There were far more
Irish in New York than in Dublin.1

De bharr na hoibre a rinne Dubhghlas de hide,2 Máirtín Ó Cadhain,3
agus Máirín Ni Mhuiríosa 4 ach go háirithe, tá tuiscint i bhfad níos fearr
anois againn ar na hiarrachtaí a rinneadh leis an Ghaeilge a
chaomhnú agus a chothú sna blianta idir an Gorta Mór agus bunú
Chonradh na Gaeilge in 1893. Cés moite de na hiarrachtaí a
rinneadh lasmuigh d'Éirinn, ba é Baile Átha Cliath lárionad na hoibre
sin. Is sa chathair sin a bunaíodh na cumainn agus a tháinig siad le
chéile agus is ann leis a foilsíodh na leabhair agus na hirisí in am
tráth. Cén t-ionadh mar sin, i bhfianaise ar scríobh an tAmbasadóir
Shannon, go mbeadh daoine go leor i Nua-Eabhrac freisin agus i
gcathracha eile i Meiriceá ar spéis leo caomhnú agus cothú na
Gaeilge? Is i an aidhm ata agam leis na nótaí seo, léargas éigin ar
feadh m’eolais a thabhairt ar ghnéithe de ghluaiseacht na Gaeilge i
Meiriceá a léireoidh ar bhealach níos soiléire go raibh reamhchon-
raitheoirí thall a bhí gach pioc chomh gníomhach leo siúd abhus,
mura raibh siad níos gníomhaí féin.

Cé nach mbaineann sé go díreach le hábhar na nótaí seo, tá sé thar
am ag duine a bheadh cáilithe chuige, scrúdú údarásach a dhéanamh
ar cháipéisí daonra na hÉireann agus na Stat Aontaithe ó aimsir an
Ghorta Mhóir i leith, féachaint an bhféadfaí lion na nGaeilgeoirí i
Meiriceá (daoine ar de bhunadh na Gaeltachta iad is mo a bheadh i
gceist anseo) ag tréimhsí éagsúla a mheas. Tá géarghá lena leithéid de
thaighde go háirithe sna Stáit Aontaithe ós rud é go bhfuil aird á
dhíriú le tamall de bhlianta anuas ar na teangacha éagsúla a labhair
agus a labhraíonn na hinimircigh sna Stáit.5 Cuireann sé díomá ar an
nGaeilgeoir a fheiceáil ar thábla a léiríonn lion na “Foreign Born
White Males Unable to Speak English, By Occupation, 1890” nár

<L 66>
áiríodh chor ar bith daoine a rugadh in Éirinn ós rud é gur glacadh
leis “that virtually all could speak English prior to emigration”,6 go
háirithe ón uair nach raibh riamh oiread daoine sna Stáit Aontaithe a
rugadh in Éirinn, agus nach mbeadh arís, agus a bhí sa bhliain ina
dhiaidh sin, 1891. Bhí beagnach dhá mhilliún ann, agus bhí níos mó
ná an dá mhilliún ann in 1891, agus ó 1871 i leith, má chuirtear
Ceanada san áireamh.7 Ós rud é gur throime i gcónaí on Ghorta Mór
i leith an imirce go Meiriceá as na contaetha in iarthar agus i
ndeisceart na hÉireann, na contaetha inar mhair an Ghaeltacht,
caithfidh sé go raibh i bhfad Éireann níos mo Gaeilgeoirí i Nua-
Eabhrac sa tréimhse atá i gceist sna nótaí seo na mar a bhí i mBaile
Átha Cliath ach is deacair a bheith cinnte ina thaobh sin ceal taighde
údarásaigh.

Orthu siúd a chuaigh ar imirce go Meiriceá sa bhliain 1871 bhí
Micheál Ó Lócháin, a bhfuil neart cainte air sa leabhar seo. É siúd a
bhunaigh an iris dhátheangach An Gaodhal agus is foinse fhíorluach-
mhar i an iris chéanna do stair ghluaiseacht na Gaeilge i Meiriceá le
linn an Lóchánaigh. Feicfear gur tharraingios go tréan as An
Gaodhal mar fhoinse dá bhfuil sna nótaí seo. Is fiú rud amháin a rá
faoin Lóchánach ag an bpointe seo áfach. In ainneoin go dtaibhreodh
sé don léitheoir inniu gur ghlór aonaráin san fhásach ab ea a
ghlórsan, is gá a rá gur amharc seisean air féin mar chuid de
ghluaiseacht—de ghluaiseacht na Gaeilge. Nil a fhios agam cén uair
ar tosaíodh ar chur síos ar obair na Gaeilge mar ghluaiseacht ach is
mar sin a thagair an Lóchánach don obair agus d’ainmnigh sé athair
na gluaiseachta céanna: ‘The anniversary of the late Archbishop
McHale, the father of the Irish Language Movement, will be cele-
brated on Thursday evening, March the 5th’ (An Gaodhal, Feabhra
1885).

NA PHILO-CELTIC SOCIETIES

Tá tábhacht faoi leith leis na Philo-Celtic Societies do stair ghluais-
eacht na Gaeilge. Nuair a bunaíodh Conradh na Gaeilge i mBaile Átha
Cliath in 1893, dearbhaíodh gur rud nua ar fad ab ea príomh-aidhm
an Chonartha, an Ghaeilge a choinneáil á labhairt i measc na ndaoine
in Éirinn. D’admhaigh an Craoibhín Aoibhinn áfach go raibh “cúpla
cumann beag Gaedhilge i Nua-Eabhrac agus i mBrooklinn, agus
b’éidir go ndearna siad-san iarracht ar Ghaedhilg do labhairt eatorra
féin. Nil mé cinnte faoi sin.”8 Is é an cumann a bhí i gceist aige i
mBrooklyn an Brooklyn Philo-Celtic Society, a bunaiodh sa bhliain
1874 agus a raibh tuairiscí rialta ar a gcuid imeachtaí ag an
Lóchánach in An Gaodhal. Is é an Lóchánach féin a d’fhág bliain
bhunaithe an chumainn againn agus freagra á thabhairt aige ar alt i

<L 67>
nuachtán laethúil faoi stair ghluaiseacht na Gaeilge i Nua-Eabhrac.
Bhí fíricí áirithe le ceartú, dar leis.

How can people place reliance on so-called history when facts
which have transpired within the last fifteen years are distorted
through personal envy? A long article appeared in the New York
Daily News a few weeks ago purporting to give the history of the
organization of the Gaelic movement in America which does not
contain one word of truth in relation to the foundation of that move-
ment, and, in confirmation of this assertion, we refer the reader to the
columns of the Irish World of 1872.

This is the History of the Irish Language Movement in America,
and it was not prompted or assisted by members of any other
society—
1872 The agitation to preserve the language commenced by Gael
(M. J. Logan) in the Irish World—The first Irish Class
organized.

1874 The Brooklyn Gaelic Class given the name, Philo-Celtic
Society.

(An Gaodhal, Bealtaine 1890)

Más rud é gur bhuan cuimhne an Lóchánaigh in 1890, bhí ar a
laghad Philo-Celtic Society amháin eile níos sine ná an ceann i
mBrooklyn. Bunaíodh an cumann, faoin ainm Philo-Celtic Associa-
tion, i mBostún ar 28 Aibreán 1873. Seo i an tuairisc a bhí san
Boston Herald an la dar gcionn:

At a meeting of the Philo-Celtic Association (organized for the
purpose of promoting the knowledge of the Celtic language), held at
176 Tremont Street last night, the following board of officers was
elected: President, P. J. Flatley; vice-president, Jeremiah Slattery;
recording secretary, P. J. Daly; corresponding secretary, Robert
Dwyer; treasurer, Michael O’Sullivan. A committee was also
appointed to draw up a constitution for the government of the society,
and also one to procure a hall for business meetings.9

Maíonn James J. Ford, atá ar fhoireann na Leabharlainne Poibli i
mBostún, gurbh é sin an chéad chumann a bunaiodh sna Stáit
Aontaithe le staidéar a dhéanamh ar an nGaeilge; nil me cinnte an do
na Philo-Celtic Societies go ginearálta a bhí sé ag tagairt nó don
“chraobh” áirithe seo i mBostún.

This was the first society established in the United States for the study
of the Irish language. The founders were all Irish speakers who had
been meeting informally for some time with Michael C. O’Shea and
P. J. Daly, both of whom had a deep knowledge of Irish. The society
was founded to enable others in the city to learn Irish. In September
1874 The Pilot noted that there was for the first time a Celtic school
<L 68>
in Boston expressely organized to give free instruction. Wittke 10 noted
that the society attracted the attention of scholars in Dublin and prob-
ably had some influence upon the formation of the Society for the Pre-
servation of the Irish language there in 1876.

On January 24, 1880 the President of the Society, Jeremiah H.
Sullivan, gave a progress report for the past year. In addition to the
central school, the Society had established Irish schools in East
Boston, South Boston, Boston Highlands, the North End, Salem,
Weymouth, and Quincy. There was also a school in Lawrence which
was inspired, but not established, by the Society. About three hun-
dred copies of Burke's Easy Lessons, O’Donovan’s Irish Grammar
and Joyce’s Irish Grammar had been sold. The Society had punches
cut and matrices made from which Irish type was cast by the Boston
Type Foundry, the first Irish type ever manufactured in the United
States. This Irish type was made available to newspapers and printers
throughout the country for the same price as Roman type.11

Níor mhair na scoileanna Gaeilge i bhfad. Faoin mbliain 1886 ni
raibh fágtha ach aon cheann amháin, an chéad cheann a bunaíodh,
ach féachadh le brainsí den scoil lair sin a bhunú arís.

The school moved in 1889 from 176 Tremont Street to Cheverus
Hall of St. James Parish at 122 Harrison Avenue. The pastor, Father
F. W. McQuaid, was deeply interested in the Irish language move-
ment. In that year twenty students were enrolled in the advanced class
and they were reading the Imitation of Christ in Irish. Branch schools
were established in Malden, Lawrence, Haverhill, Worcester and in
Manchester, New Hampshire. In 1894 a school was established in
Providence, Rhode Island and the John Boyle O’Reilly Literary
Association in Boston started an Irish class with P. J. Daly as
teacher. In addition to O’Daly, the following were listed as having
taught at the central Boston school over the years: D. W. Cody,
Patrick A. Collins, Mary J. Donovan, Miss E. J. Granger, Mary
Hynes, Miss D. Lyons, P. Mac Sweeney, M. J. Mulroy, Michael C.
O’Shea, Daniel O’Sullivan, P. J. Sullivan and Timothy Sullivan.12

Ábhar iontais dom, i bhfianaise a bhfuil thuas, go bhféadfadh an
Craoibhín Aoibhinn a rá faoin chuairt a thug sé ar Bhostún in 1891,
“Dá mbeadh aon rud, beag nó mór, d’á dhéanamh ar son na
Gaedhilge i mBoston an uair sin, 1890 (recte 189113), is dóigh go
bhfeicfinn é, acht ní fhacas éinne do chuir suim ar bith innti …”.14
Ceachtar de dhá mhíniú atá agamsa: an féidir, faoin am ar chrom sé
ar a chuid cuimhní cinn a scríobh daichead bliain ina dhiaidh sin, go
raibh imeachtaí na Gaeilge i mBostún ligthe i ndearmad aige, nó an
amhlaidh go díreach nár luaigh duine ar bith i mBostún leis iomlán a
raibh déanta ar son na Gaeilge acu sa chathair sin ó 1873 i leith?
<L 69>
Feicfear ón chéad mhír eile de na nótaí seo go bhfuil suim faoi leith
agam féin in imeacht áirithe amháin Gaeilge a tharla i mí na Samhna
1884. Is mó an t-eolas mar sin atá agam ar imeachtaí na Gaeilge i
Meiriceá i ngeimhreadh na bliana 1884-85 ná in am ar bith eile. Ó
thuairiscí éagsúla san Irish World agus in An Gaodhal an
geimhreadh sin, feicim go raibh Philo-Celtic Societies ag feidhmiú i
mBrooklyn, i mBostún, i Philadelphia, i Nua-Eabhrac agus i San
Francisco. Gheofar léargas ar imeachtaí na gcumann éagsúla i
dtuairiscí sa nuachtán agus san iris sin.

Brooklyn P.-C.S.

Tá sampla de stíl throdach scríbhneoireachta Mhíchíl Uí Lócháin
feicthe cheana féin againn agus tá neart eile ar an dtéad céanna san
alt leis an Ollamh Breandán 0 Buachalla. Mar a léirigh an Craoibhín,
bhí an Lóchánach “beo bríomhar” ach d’fhéadfadh sé a bheith
“bríomhar borb” chomh maith. Scríobh sé a lán lán nótaí beaga faoi
imeachtaí agus faoi bhaill an Philo-Celtic Society i mBrooklyn san
iris. Mhol sé baill a bhí i láthair go rialta agus a bhí in am; chain sé
daoine nach raibh in am nó a bhíodh as láthair. Spreag sé baill chun
breis dúthrachta i bhfoghlaim na Gaeilge; chuir sé comhairle a leasa
ar dhaoine eile. Line nó dhó a bhíodh sna nótaí seo de ghnáth, ach ó
am go ham thug sé léacht uaidh nuair a cheap sé go raibh léacht
tuillte. Bheadh amhras an-mhór orm faoi fhiúntas na nótaí seo le baill
a mhealladh le fanacht sa chumann. Sna nótaí seo leis, creidim go
léirítear bunlaigeacht na gcumann seo mar áiseanna leis an Ghaeilge
féin a chaomhnú no a fhorbairt. Bhí na cumainn seo ag freastal ar
dhá thrá, ar dhaoine ar spéis leo an Ghaeilge agus ar dhaoine eile
b’fhéidir ar spéis leo an ceol; níor ghá gur cheol Gaelach a bhí i gceist
ach oiread ach ceol Éireannach an pharlúis.

Costello, Miss Nora T. Costello is back from the country, so that
our Gaelic friends will have the pleasure of some patriotic songs in
their native purity.

Cassidy, Brother Cassidy wants to swallow Irish all at once. He is
a studious student and cannot fail, in time, to obtain his wish.
However, as brother Cassidy has read the Elements of Euclid we
would call his attention to that eminent geomatrician's reply to
Ptolemy Philadelphus in relation to the study of mathematics.15

Woods, The foregoing remarks apply, also, to brother Woods.
[The Society is attracting to itself the culture, intelligence and
patriotism of our Irish-American citizens.]

Murray, We cannot praise the Treasurer, Miss Murray, for regular
attendance lately. Why?

Dunning. Also brother Dunning.

(An Gaodhal, Nollaig 1884)

<L 70>
The Brooklyn Philo-Celtic Society is getting along well, and the
latest item of news in connection with it is the marriage of the
Treasurer, Miss Fanny O’N. Murray, to Mr. Dunning, a brother
member. The Gael wishes them all the happiness attainable in this
world, and hopes they will not, under this new order of affairs, forget,
as a good many of their predecessors, under similar circumstances,
have done, the occasion of their acquaintance—the preservation and
cultivation of their mother tongue. —And that they will continue to
give the movement that care and attention so necessary to its full
development.

President Finn has not put in an appearance in quite a while.

Miss Donnelly is very attentive—she sung “Believe me if all” etc.
the other evening with immense effect.

Miss Nelly Crowley spoke an elegant piece the other night.
Speaking seems to be Miss Crowley’s forte.

Brother Graham is also on time and very attentive to the classes, in
fact, the principal instructor.

(An Gaodhal, Eanáir 1885)

Ex-President Gilgannon spoke a few encouraging words, in Irish,
to the members the other night.
Costello—We are pleased to see Miss Nora T. Costello back again
to charm the society with her rendition of Moore’s Melodies.
Ward—Miss Ward is an expert Gaelic scholar.
Dunning—We presume brother Dunning and his bride will attend
better after the honeymoon.
Flaherty—Sergeant at arms Flaherty is absent quite often.
Erley—Brother Erley, of N Y S P I L, called and paid a visit a few
evenings ago.

(An Gaodhal, Feabhra 1885)

Boston P.-C.S.

We understand that the Boston Philo-Celtic Society are about to
start a Journal partly in Irish and English—We wish them every
success, and we shall do all in our power to publish the fact. We won’t
be like the “Dog in the Manger.” We have no interest in the Gael
apart from the cultivation of the language. Our Boston friends did not
communicate their intention to us, perhaps for the reason that we
might not wish to see a journal in opposition to the Gael—If that be
the cause, our Boston friends mistook their man. We would like to see
such journals in every town and city, and would advertise them too,
and that gratuitously. Perhaps they took us to be like the proprietors
of other Irish-American journals who never mention the Gael unless
in dispraise lest it should take a cent out of their pockets. No, no,
friends, we shall take as much pride in your success in Boston as if
you were a part and parcel of the Gael in Brooklyn. We saw the
notice in the Irish World, with the election of the board of officers as
follows—

Philadelphia P.-C.S.

The Philo-Celtic Society of Philadelphia will give an entertainment on
Nov. 26 at the City Institute Hall, 18th and Chestnut streets.
Speeches in the Irish language and music will be the features. The
proceeds are for the benefit of the Irish School of Spring Garden
street.

(Irish World, 29 Samhain 1884)

On Nov. 26, a grand Irish musical and Literary entertainment was
given under the auspices of the Philo-Celtic Society at the Institute
Hall, Philadelphia. The attendance was large and enthusiastic. The
programme consisted of Irish and American national songs,
recitations, choruses, etc., and all who took part acquitted them-
selves most creditably. The Philadelphia Society is working
strenuously to preserve the old tongue and their every effort in this
direction deserves the heartiest support.

(Irish World, 13 Nollaig 1884)

New York P.-C.S.

The New York P.C. Society had a very successful entertainment a
few evenings since, but that is no wonder under the direction of their
energetic President, Mr. Meeres. The Hon. Denis Burns recited with
great effect the dialogue between Andromache and Hector, in Irish.
(An Gaodhal, Nollaig 1884)

California P.-C.S. (San Francisco)

The Philo-Celtic Society held its first annual meeting recently at 871
Market street, Patrick McGrael presiding and Thomas Brennan
Secretary. The society has made rapid progress. There are now
between 50 and 60 members, all of whom are learning the Irish
tongue well and some of whom speak it as fluently as English. The
election of officers resulted as follows:— Pres., J. J. Cooney; Vice
do., Jeremiah Deasy; Rec. Sec., Lawrence Fitzpatrick; Fin. Sec., M.

marc 06-02-2020
<L 72>
The Brooklyn Philo-Celtic Society is getting along well, and the
latest item of news in connection with it is the marriage of the
Treasurer, Miss Fanny O’N. Murray, to Mr. Dunning, a brother
member. The Gael wishes them all the happiness attainable in this
world, and hopes they will not, under this new order of affairs, forget,
as a good many of their predecessors, under similar circumstances,
have done, the occasion of their acquaintance—the preservation and
cultivation of their mother tongue. —  And that they will continue to
give the movement that care and attention so necessary to its full
development.

President Finn has not put in an appearance in quite a while.
Miss Donnelly is very attentive—she sung “Believe me if all” etc.
the other evening with immense effect.
Miss Nelly Crowley spoke an elegant piece the other night.
Speaking seems to be Miss Crowley’s forte.
Brother Graham is also on time and very attentive to the classes, in
fact, the principal instructor.
(An Gaodhal, Eanáir 1885)

Ex-President Gilgannon spoke a few encouraging words, in Irish,
to the members the other night.
Costello—We are pleased to see Miss Nora T. Costello back again
to charm the society with her rendition of Moore’s Melodies.
Ward—Miss Ward is an expert Gaelic scholar.
Dunning—We presume brother Dunning and his bride will attend
better after the honeymoon.
Flaherty—Sergeant at arms Flaherty is absent quite often.
Erley—Brother Erley, of N Y S P I L, called and paid a visit a few
evenings ago.
(An Gaodhal, Feabhra 1885)

Boston P.-C.S.

We understand that the Boston Philo-Celtic Society are about to
start a Journal partly in Irish and English—We wish them every
success, and we shall do all in our power to publish the fact. We won’t
be like the “Dog in the Manger.” We have no interest in the Gael
apart from the cultivation of the language. Our Boston friends did not
communicate their intention to us, perhaps for the reason that we
might not wish to see a journal in opposition to the Gael—If that be
the cause, our Boston friends mistook their man. We would like to see
such journals in every town and city, and would advertise them too,
and that gratuitously. Perhaps they took us to be like the proprietors
of other Irish-American journals who never mention the Gael unless
in dispraise lest it should take a cent out of their pockets. No, no,
friends, we shall take as much pride in your success in Boston as if
you were a part and parcel of the Gael in Brooklyn. We saw the
notice in the Irish World, with the election of the board of officers as
follows—
<L 71>
Prest., P. J. Conlan, re-elected; vice-Prests., M. T. Gallivan, Mary
A. Mahoney; Rec. Sec. T. M. O’Sullivan, re-elected; Fin. Sec. W. M.
Murphy, re-elected; Cor. Sec. P. J. O’Daly, re-elected; Treas. P. J.
Sullivan, re-elected; Trustees, J. O’Neill, D. S. Hart and Mary
Garvey, re-elected.

(An Gaodhal, Samhain 1884)

Nil tuairim dá laghad agam cén fáth ar theastaigh na Sergeants-at-

Bhí an oiread sin cumainn éagsúla sna Stáit Aontaithe sa bhliain
1884 ag obair le leas na Gaeilge gur rith an smaoineamh le Micheál Ó
Lócháin gur mhithid Comhdháil Náisiúnta de chairde na Gaeilge a
reachtáil. Thaitin an smaoineamh leis an Philo-Celtic Society i Phila-
delphia agus mhol siadsan go bhfoilseofaí treoirliosta de na cumainn
éagsúla. D'fhoilsigh an Lóchánach pé eolas a bhí aige féin i dtosach
na bliana 1885:

Mr. McFadden of the Phila. P.C.S. says,—The question that has
been spoken of several times in the Gael of a National Convention of
the friends of the Irish Language should be carried out this coming
Summer or Fall; it would be productive of a great deal of good to the
cause. If not asking too much, I would suggest publishing a directory
of the time and place of meeting of the various schools for the benefit
of travelling readers of the Gael while visiting distant cities or towns,
that they may have an opportunity of meeting friends engaged in the
same cause.— The Phila. S. meet at Philopatrian Hall, every Sunday,
visiting friends are invited to call.

We think this a good idea, and hope the heads of societies will
furnish the desired information for said directory. The Brooklyn S.
meets at Jefferson hall, opposite the Court-house, Thursday and
Sunday evenings throughout the year, hail or snow, from half past
seven till half past nine. The New York S.P.I. Language at Claren-
don Hall, 114 E. 13th. Sundays from 3 to 5 p.m., P. Morrissey
President. N Y Philo Celtic Society, 295 Bowery, Sundays 3 to 5,
Tuesdays 7 to 10, M. Meeres President. We have no official notice of
other meetings and therefore cannot record.

We have been advised from Chicago that a Gaelic Society has been
organized there which purposes (sic) leaving all existing societies in
the shade. We promise, on behalf of Brooklyn, that such is easier said
than done; in the meantime we welcome the friendly rivalry.

(An Gaodhal, Feabhra 1885)

S.P.I.L. I NUA-EABHRAC

Mar atá ar eolas go coitianta, is ag deireadh na bliana 1876, ar an

<L 73>
29 Nollaig, a bunaíodh an Society for the Preservation of the Irish
Language i mBaile Átha Cliath.18 Lá le Pádraig ina dhiaidh sin
(1877) leagadh amach cuspóirí an chumainn. An bhliain ina dhiaidh
sin arís, in 1878, cé nach eol dom go baileach cén uair sa bhliain,
bunaíodh an New York Society for the Preservation of the Irish
Language. Níorbh ionann go díreach aidhmeanna an da chumann.

Ón chéad lá ní hé amháin gur chuir an cumann i Nua-Eabhrac
béim ar an nGaeilge féin, ar ranganna Gaeilge agus ar shaothrú na
litríochta, ach bhí suim ar leith acu sa cheol. I mBaile Átha Cliath,
bhí mar aidhm ag an gcumann “the Preservation and Extension of
the Irish as a Spoken Language” 19; i Nua-Eabhrac bhí na
haidhmeanna ni ba leithne agus ni ba uaillmhianaí, b’fhéidir, “to
promote the study of the Irish Language; to Preserve it as a Spoken
Tongue; to Encourage the Study and Dissemination of Irish
Literature; to cultivate and practice the National Music of Ireland,
and to establish in the City of New York an Irish Literary
Institute.”  20 Ba ghearr go raibh cáil ar na “Irish festivals” a
reachtáladh faoi choimirce an S.P.I.L. i Nua-Eabhrac; mi na Samhna
1884 reachtáladh ceann de na cinn ba mhó agus go cinnte an ceann
ba tábhachtaí do stair litríocht na Gaeilge.

Is amhlaidh a d’iarr an S.P.I.L. i Nua-Eabhrac ar Phól Mac
Suibhne (Paul Mac Swiney), ceoltóir óg as Corcaigh a chuaigh ar
.imirce go Nua-Eabhrac am éigin idir 1880 agus 1884, saothar
drámata Gaeilge le ceol a chumadh, idir fhocail agus cheol. Léirigh
baill an chumainn féin an ceoldráma ar cheann de phríomhfhéilte
Mheiriceá (Thanksgiving .i. an ceathrú Déardaoin i mi na Samhna)
agus d'fhoilsigh an cumann libretto an tsaothair i nGaeilge agus i
mBéarla i bhfoirm phaimfléid.21 Léiríodh an saothar seo faoi dhó,
Déardaoin agus Dé hAoine 27 agus 28 Samhain 1884, i nGaeilge an
chéad oíche agus i mBéarla an dara hoíche, i halla leis an gcomhlacht
cáiliúil déanta piano, Steinway, agus foilsíodh léirmheasanna ar na
nuachtáin mhóra laethúla i Nua-Eabhrac (New York Daily Tribune,
New York Herald agus New York Times) ar 28 Samhain agus san
Irish World agus in An Gaodhal go gairid ina dhiaidh sin.

Tugadh neart poiblíochta don fhiontar seo roimh re chomh maith.
Bhí fógraí sna nuachtáin laethúla agus san Irish World maille le alt
speisialta bolscaireachta san Irish World ag tathaint ar dhaoine a
dhul ann. Is spéisiúil a fheiceáil san alt seo an ceangal a bhí in intinn
an scríbhneora idir obair chur chun cinn na Gaeilge agus an cheoil
agus féinmheas na nGael agus a gcearta chun náisiúntachta.

IRISH MUSIC IN STEINWAY HALL
First Production of MacSwiney’s Gaelic Idyll,
“An Bard ’gus an Fo”
Next Thursday evening (Thanksgiving) the chorus of the Society

<L 74>
for the Preservation of the Irish Language produce for the first time in
Steinway Hall a new Irish musical work, entitled “An Bard ’gus an
Fo”, which has been specially composed for the occasion by a
talented young Irish musician, Paul McSwiney. Arrangements for its
production have been perfected to insure a good performance of what
will prove an enjoyable programme. The members of the Society are
adepts in anything they undertake. Their present enterprise will be one
of the most unique entertainments ever held in New York.

The composition, words, and music, is very highly spoken of by
those competent to judge.

The four characters of the piece will be represented as follows:
“Nessa”, Miss Carrie Hun-King; “Bania”, Miss Clara A. Stuttsman;
“Beothach, a Soldier”, Mr. Wm. Long; “Eocaidh, a Bard”, Mr. P. S.
Munro, with full chorus and orchestra.

Cards of admission, costing fifty cents each, are now for sale at the
box office in Steinway Hall. The doors will open at 7.30, and the per-
formance commences punctually at 8 o’clock p.m., Thanksgiving
night. Those present at any of the famous Irish festivals of this society
need no urging to make them attend anything undertaken by the
society. To all others who take delight in listening to the music of their
native land, when it is interpreted by those competent to give expres-
sion to all the grandeur of its pathos and feeling, we say go, hear and
see for yourself numbers that will awaken within your hearts
memories long slumbering of many a pleasant seanachas at the fire-
side of your own homes. Aside, though, from every other considera-
tion this society deserves well of the Irish public; its work on behalf of
our old language and music is worthy of every praise and all the
encouragement which can be rendered by every one interested in
seeing the speech of their race assume its rightful place in all the
educational institutions of the world. The more our language, history,
and music are understood the more claim can we lay on the educated
mind to advance every struggle made for Ireland. Therefore, it
behooves all to assist in the presentation of what promises to be but
the inception of a series of Irish festivals that will unquestionably
prove of immense value in gaining for Ireland the attention of the
public mind, which, once interested, is sure to inquire further into all
the essentials we possess to fit us to enjoy the rights of nationality. To
achieve it, as well as to maintain it in its integrity, is the work before
all of us. Let us show by our presence in Steinway Hall on Thanks-
giving evening that we stand ready to do our share toward its
accomplishment.

(Irish World, 29 Samhain 1884)22

<L 76>

“An Bárd 'gus An Fó” 

Sgéul Ghaedhlach.

Gan cuimhne atá na baird do bhí,
Gan cuimhne a sgeulta grádh a choidh'e;
Gan cuimhne an cath-rann treun, ársuigh,
'gus an cronán ós cionn na marbh;
Óir thánaic faol-chú an t-Sasanuigh,
A's tá cluinte a uaill go garbh.

Bhí deora 'gus teine 'nn ar d-teangain bhreágh;
In gach focal budh fúaire bhí abhrán ghrádh.
Á Dhia! mar faisgeadh gach croidhe le crádh,
Nuair chualaidh siad gul caointeaóra!
Acht Ó! mar do ghleusadh gach teud an trá
do sheinn sé air Eirinn 's a glóire!

Á! bhí ceol san t-Sean-Tír 'san am fad ó,
Nuair bhí grádh i g-croidhthibh fear níos teó,
'gus bhí deunta bearta i g-cath 'sa n-gleó
Do b'fhiú iad abhráin fíre.
'Gus bhí ceoltóiridhe binne an trá sin beó
Do mholfadh near na tíre.

Leathanaigh a 6 agus a 7 den leabhrán.
A GAELIC IDYLL.

PROLOGUE.

Forgotten are they, the Bards of Old,
Forgotten the tales of love they told;
Forgotten the war-song, stern and bold,
And the lullaby o’er the dead;
For the Saxon wolf hath crept on the fold,
And his voice is heard instead.

There were tears and fire in that melting tongue,
Whose coldest word was a soulful song.
Ah God ! how iron hearts were wrung
By the wail of the Keener haunted I
But Oh I how their chords again were strung
When the Might of the Dead he chanted I

Ah! music lived in the Old Land then,
When love made hearts, and hearts made men,
And men wrought deeds again and again
Were worthy a minstrel’s song,

And minstrels there were whose immortal strain
Could no mortal prowess wrong.
<L 77>
bliana. Ní mór an plota a thabhairt anseo mar áis leis na léir-
mheasanna a thuiscint.

Tosaítear le “Brollach”; ansin cuirtear ceann ar Pháirt I le
“Cóimhsheinm Osgailteach”. Castar beirt fhear ar a chéile, Eocaidh,
bard ón Deisceart, agus Beothach, fó,24 nó saighdiúir, ón Tuaisceart.
Deir Eocaidh faoin áit inar rugadh é féin: “Insin chois na trágh’ do
fuaireas mo laoigh”. Geallann an bheirt go mbeidh siad dílis dá chéile
agus nach dtiocfaidh bean ar bith eatarthu.

Acht, taobh le taobh go deó
A síothcháin nó a n-gleó,
Béidhmíd le chéile beó
In oidh’e ’gus ló.

Tagann beirt chailín i láthair ansin, Nessa agus Bania, agus
tarlaíonn mórán an rud céanna dóibhsean. Ansin castar an ceathrar
acu ar a chéile agus cantar “Cóimhsheinm” eile in a dtugtar le fios go
mbristear na geallúintí.

’Nois do shuaimhnigh na seachránuidhe saor ó gach brón;—
Ó Fhir na h-Éireann!
A’s bhí geallta na maidne faoi dhearmad roimh nóin;—
Ó Mhná na h-Éireann!
Óir do mhúsgail an Bárd, le n-a cheól binn, árd,
Mac-alla chómh-fhulaing na mná;
Acht an Saighdiúr fíor, le abhrán saor
Do las sé a súil le grádh.

Nil sé soiléir ón téacs cad a tharlaíonn ina dhiaidh sin. Is cosúil go
dtógann Beothach Nessa ar leathtaobh leis an áit a bhfuil cónaí air a
thaispeáint di (“a g-croidhe na coille”); tugann seo deis d’Eocaidh
“sean sgeul” a sheinm do Bhania. Ina dhiaidh sin cantar “Cath-
Rann” (“War Song”) agus cuirtear deireadh le Páirt I de “An Bard
’gus an Fó” agus iad ar fad ag imeacht le cath a throid i mball éigin.
Gan amhras, is chun saoirse agus cearta na hÉireann a chuirtear an
cath; ní deirtear cé hi an namhaid.

Nil i bPáirt II, faoi mar atá sé i gcló, ach sraith amhrán. Tosaitear
le “Paidir”, ansin amhrán ó Bhania, “Ní’l agam sógh, ni’l agam
sioth” (“I know not peace, I know not rest”), agus “Abhrán chum
Éin” (“Song to a Bird”) ó Nessa.

Tar chugam! tar chugam!
’Gus cómhnuidhe annso faoi sgáth!
Óir chugad, Ó chugad,
Ní feidir liom dul go bráth.
Cá bh-fuil tú? Cá bh-fuil tú
Aig eit’lladh air árd ’san aer?
<L 78>
Tar chugam! tar chugam!
A’s seinn dam do cheól go saor!

Ina dhiaidh sin, canann Eocaidh agus Beothach amhrán eile,
“Marsháil na m-Buaidhteóiridh aig filleadh ó’n g-Cath” (“March of
Victors returning from battle”). Ansin, tar éis don cheathrar acu
saghas lúibiní a chanadh dá chéile, críochnaitear le “Cóimhsheinm”
eile.

Nuair in Earrach a saoghail tá dhá fhior-chroidhe mar aon:—
Ó Fhir na h-Éireann!
A m-bhláth deas an tSamhraidh béidh fios air a seun:—
Ó Mhná na h-Éireann!
Acht ’san Fhóghmhar bhuidhe, nuair tá’n fallsa faoi,
Mar dhúillibh críon’ na m-bláth;
Béidh siadsan treun, go síntear iad ’raon,
’San Roillig fhuar go bráth.

Is léir go raibh dul amú ar pé duine a scríobh an t-alt san Irish
World nuair a dúirt sé go gcuirfeadh “An Bárd ’gus an Fó”
oícheanta seanchais sa bhaile in Éirinn i gcuimhne don lucht
éisteachta. Saothar sofaisticiúil ba ea é seo a d’eascair as tuiscinti an
údair ar thraidisiún na ceoldrámaíochta éadroime agus an cheoil
chlasaicigh. Is dócha gur thaitin sé le daoine a raibh na tuiscintí
céanna sin acu féin, ach silim gur cheap Micheál Ó Lócháin, ainneoin
bród an domhain a bheith air a leithéid a fheiceáil ar ardán i Nua-
Eabhrac, go raibh sé róshofaisticiúil don ghnáthdhuine i ngluaiseacht
na Gaeilge thall. Bhí deacrachtaí eile ag baint leis an léiriú. Mar a
tharlaíonn go minic i gcás a leithéid d’fhiontar, dealraíonn sé nach
raibh duine de na hamhránaithe a fógraíodh in ann a bheith páirteach
agus tháinig fear eile ina áit. (William Long a fógraíodh a bheadh i
bpáirt Bheothaigh; de réir na dtuairiscí, is é Joseph J. Kilduff a bhí sa
pháirt.) Chomh maith leis sin, bheadh amhras orm faoi chumas an
lucht éisteachta mórán meabhrach a bhaint as a raibh le rá nó le
canadh i nGaeilge ag Clara Stuttsman nó ag Carrie Hun King. Ar
ndóigh, ní féidir a bheith cinnte de sin; cá bhfios nár Ghael-Ghear-
mánach i Carrie, le tuismitheoir amháin de Chlann Attila agus tuis-
mitheoir eile de Chlann Mhic Con Ri as Conamara!
